					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Production model to minimise wastage and emissions</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1305433154">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" for suggestions.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Linia_kontra%C5%AD_Cirkulero.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Linia_kontra%C5%AD_Cirkulero.svg/500px-Linia_kontra%C5%AD_Cirkulero.svg.png" decoding="async" width="340" height="157" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Linia_kontra%C5%AD_Cirkulero.svg/960px-Linia_kontra%C5%AD_Cirkulero.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="911" data-file-height="421" /></a><figcaption>Contrast between the wasteful "take, make, dispose" approach of a linear economy, and that of a circular economy</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Circular economy</b> (CE), also referred to as <b>circularity</b>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a model of resource <a href="/wiki/Production_(economics)" title="Production (economics)">production</a> and <a href="/wiki/Resource_consumption" title="Resource consumption">consumption</a> that involves <a href="/wiki/Sharing" title="Sharing">sharing</a>, leasing, <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">reusing</a>, repairing, refurbishing, and <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> materials and products, to extend product life cycle for as long as possible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-europarl.europa.eu&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl.europa.eu-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The concept aims to tackle global challenges such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> by emphasizing the design-based implementation of the three base principles of the model. The main three principles required for the transformation to a circular economy are:
</p>
<ul><li>designing out waste and pollution,</li>
<li>keeping products and materials in use, and</li>
<li>regenerating natural systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Circular_Economy_concept.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/The_Circular_Economy_concept.png/500px-The_Circular_Economy_concept.png" decoding="async" width="340" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/The_Circular_Economy_concept.png/960px-The_Circular_Economy_concept.png 2x" data-file-width="1649" data-file-height="841" /></a><figcaption>In a circular economy, items that would be wastefully discarded can instead be sent back to an earlier point in the construction process<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer-2020&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer-2020-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure> 
<p>Circular economy is defined in contradistinction to the traditional linear economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;to&#95;circular&#95;economy&#95;overview&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_to_circular_economy_overview-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reuter&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuter-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The idea and concepts of a circular economy have been studied extensively in academia, business, and government over the past ten years. It has been gaining popularity because it can help to minimize <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">carbon emissions</a> and the consumption of raw materials, open up new market prospects, and, principally, increase the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_consumption" title="Sustainable consumption">sustainability of consumption</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At a government level, a circular economy is viewed as a method of combating <a href="https://simple.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_warming" class="extiw" title="simple:Global warming">global warming</a>, as well as a facilitator of long-term growth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Circular economy may geographically connect actors and resources to stop material loops at the regional level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In its core principle, the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> defines the circular economy as "a model of production and consumption that involves sharing, leasing, reusing, repairing, refurbishing, and recycling existing materials and products as long as possible. In this way, the life cycle of products is extended".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-europarl.europa.eu&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl.europa.eu-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Global implementation of circular economy can reduce global emissions by 22.8 billion tons, equivalent to 39% of global emissions produced in 2019.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Circle&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Circle-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By implementing circular economy strategies in five sectors alone: <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>, 9.3 billion metric tons of CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> equivalent (equal to all current emissions from transportation) can be reduced.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ellenmacarthurfoundation.org&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ellenmacarthurfoundation.org-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-EIB-2023&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIB-2023-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a circular economy, business models play a crucial role in enabling the shift from linear to circular processes. Various business models have been identified that support circularity, including product-as-a-service, sharing platforms, and product life extension models, among others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These models aim to optimize resource utilization, reduce waste, and create value for businesses and customers alike, while contributing to the overall goals of the circular economy.
</p><p>Businesses can also make the transition to the circular economy, where holistic adaptations in firms' business models are needed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The implementation of circular economy principles often requires new visions and strategies and a fundamental redesign of product concepts, service offerings, and channels towards long-life solutions, resulting in the so-called 'circular business models'.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" /></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definition">Definition</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><p>
There are many definitions of and approaches to the circular economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kirchherr&#95;221–232&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchherr_221–232-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/China%27s_circular_economy" title="China&#39;s circular economy">China</a>, the circular economy is promoted as a top-down national political objective, meanwhile for the European Union, Japan, and the USA, it is a tool to design bottom-up environmental and waste management policies. The ultimate goal of promoting circular economy is the decoupling of environmental pressure from economic growth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ghisellini&#95;11–32&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ghisellini_11–32-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A comprehensive definition could be: "Circular economy is an economic system that targets zero waste and pollution throughout materials lifecycles, from environment extraction to industrial transformation, and final consumers, applying to all involved ecosystems. Upon its lifetime end, materials return to either an industrial process or, in the case of a treated organic residual, safely back to the environment as in a natural regenerating cycle. It operates by creating value at the macro, meso, and micro levels and exploiting to the fullest the <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> nested concept. Used energy sources are clean and renewable. Resource use and consumption are efficient. Government agencies and responsible consumers play an active role in ensuring the correct system long-term operation."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> </p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm/250px--Circular_Economy_Explained.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="299" data-mwtitle="Circular_Economy_Explained.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Circular_Economy_Explained.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm.1080p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="1080p.vp9.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm.144p.mjpeg.mov" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/57/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm/Circular_Economy_Explained.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /></video></span><figcaption>Circular economy explained</figcaption></figure><p>More generally, circular development is a model of economic, social, and environmental production and consumption that aims to build an autonomous and sustainable society in tune with the issue of environmental resources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-africa.iclei.org&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-africa.iclei.org-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The circular economy aims to transform the economy into one that is regenerative. An economy that innovates to reduce waste and the ecological and environmental impact of industries prior to happening, rather than waiting to address the consequences of these issues.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is done by designing new processes and solutions for the optimization of resources, decoupling reliance on finite resources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-africa.iclei.org&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-africa.iclei.org-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The circular economy is a framework of three principles, driven by design: eliminating waste and pollution, keeping products and materials in use, and regenerating natural systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;to&#95;circular&#95;economy&#95;overview&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_to_circular_economy_overview-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other definitions and precise thresholds that separate linear from circular activity have also been developed in the economic literature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kirchherr&#95;221–232&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirchherr_221–232-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ghisellini&#95;11–32&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ghisellini_11–32-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a <b>linear economy</b>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> are turned into products that are ultimately destined to become waste because of the way they have been designed and manufactured. This process is often summarized as "take, make, waste".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By contrast, a circular economy aims to transition from a 'take-make-waste' approach to a more restorative and regenerative system. It employs <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">reuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharing_economy" title="Sharing economy">sharing</a>, repair, refurbishment, <a href="/wiki/Remanufacturing" title="Remanufacturing">remanufacturing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> to create a closed-loop system, reducing the use of <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource inputs</a> and the creation of <a href="/wiki/Waste_minimisation" title="Waste minimisation">waste</a>, pollution, and carbon emissions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer&#95;757–768&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer_757–768-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The circular economy aims to keep products, materials, equipment, and infrastructure<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Invernizzi2020&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Invernizzi2020-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in use for longer, thus improving the productivity of these resources. Waste materials and energy should become input for other processes through <a href="/wiki/Waste_valorization" title="Waste valorization">waste valorization</a>: either as a component for another industrial process or as regenerative resources for nature (e.g., compost). The Ellen MacArthur Foundation (EMF) defines the circular economy as an industrial economy that is restorative or regenerative by value and design.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-EMF-2012&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMF-2012-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Circular economy strategies can be applied at various scales, from individual products and services to entire industries and cities. For example, industrial symbiosis is a strategy where waste from one industry becomes an input for another, creating a network of resource exchange and reducing waste, pollution, and resource consumption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similarly, circular cities aim to integrate circular principles into urban planning and development, foster local resource loops, and promote sustainable lifestyles among their citizens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Less than 10% of economic activity worldwide in 2022 and 2023 is circular.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EIB-2023&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIB-2023-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Anyiego-2022&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anyiego-2022-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Every year, the global population uses approximately 100 billion tonnes of materials, with more than 90% of them being wasted. The circular economy seeks to address this by eliminating waste entirely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Anyiego-2022&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anyiego-2022-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History_and_aims">History and aims</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History and aims"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The concept of a circular economy cannot be traced back to one single date or author.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The concept can be linked to various schools of thought, including <a href="/wiki/Industrial_ecology" title="Industrial ecology">industrial ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomimicry" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomimicry">biomimicry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cradle-to-cradle_design" title="Cradle-to-cradle design">cradle-to-cradle design</a> principles. Industrial ecology is the study of material and energy flows through industrial systems, which forms the basis of the circular economy. Biomimicry involves emulating nature's time-tested patterns and strategies in designing human systems. Cradle-to-cradle design is a holistic approach to designing products and systems that considers their entire life cycle, from raw material extraction to end-of-life disposal, and seeks to minimize waste and maximize resource efficiency. These interrelated concepts contribute to the development and implementation of the circular economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the end half of the 19th century, an early major effort to promote ideas now labeled the circular economy was headed by the Society for the Encouragement of Arts, Manufactures and Commerce [now the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Royal Society of Arts">Royal Society of Arts</a>].&#160;As documented by economic geographer Pierre Desrochers, RSA contributors argued that the profit motive, long-distance trade, and actors now largely absent from present-day discussions (e.g., waste dealers and brokers) promoted the creation of ever more value out of manufacturing and other residuals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1968 book <i>General System Theory</i>, biologist <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Bertalanffy" title="Ludwig von Bertalanffy">Ludwig von Bertalanffy</a>, considers growth and energy for open and closed state systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This theory was then applied to other areas, such as, in the case of the circular economy, economics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Economist <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_E._Boulding" title="Kenneth E. Boulding">Kenneth E. Boulding</a>, in his 1966 paper "The Economics of the Coming Spaceship Earth", argued that a circular economic system is a prerequisite for the maintenance of the sustainability of human life on Earth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aggeri&#95;2020&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aggeri_2020-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Boulding describes the so-called "cowboy economy" as an open system in which the natural environment is typically perceived as limitless: no limit exists on the capacity of the outside to supply or receive energy and material flows.
</p><p>In the 1981 book <i>Jobs for Tomorrow: The Potential for Substituting Manpower for Energy</i>, <a href="/wiki/Walter_R._Stahel" title="Walter R. Stahel">Walter R. Stahel</a> and Geneviève Reday-Mulvey lay the foundation for the principles of the circular economy by describing how increasing labour may reduce energy intensive activities.
</p><p>Simple economic models have ignored economy-environment interrelationships. Allan Kneese in "The Economics of Natural Resources" indicates how resources are not endlessly renewable.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1990 book <i>Economics of Natural Resources and the Environment</i>, Pearce and Turner explain the shift from the traditional linear or open-ended economic system to the circular economic system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They describe an economic system where waste at extraction, production, and consumption stages is turned into inputs.
</p><p>In the early 2000s, <a href="/wiki/China%27s_Circular_Economy" class="mw-redirect" title="China&#39;s Circular Economy">China</a> integrated the notion into its industrial and environmental policies to make them resource-oriented, production-oriented, waste-oriented, use-oriented, and life cycle-oriented.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Ellen MacArthur Foundation <sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> was instrumental in the diffusion of the concept in Europe and the Americas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Press&#95;Corner&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Corner-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, the concept of circular economy started to become popular internationally after the publication of several reports.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aggeri&#95;2020&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aggeri_2020-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The European Union introduced its vision of the circular economy in 2014, with a New Circular Economy Action Plan launched in 2020 that "shows the way to a climate-neutral, competitive economy of empowered consumers".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Press&#95;Corner&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Corner-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The original diffusion of the notion benefited from three major events: the explosion of raw material prices between 2000 and 2010, the Chinese control of rare earth materials, and the 2008 economic crisis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ecologie.gouv.fr&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecologie.gouv.fr-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Today, the climate emergency and environmental challenges induce companies and individuals in rethink their production and consumption patterns. The circular economy is framed as one of the answers to these challenges. Key macro-arguments in favour of the circular economy are that it could enable economic growth that does not add to the burden on natural resource extraction but decouples resource uses from the development of economic welfare for a growing population, reduces foreign dependence on critical materials, lowers CO<sub>2</sub> emissions, reduces waste production, and introduces new modes of production and consumption able to create further value.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-europarl.europa.eu&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl.europa.eu-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Corporate arguments in favour of the circular economy are that it could secure the supply of raw materials, reduce the price volatility of inputs and control costs, reduce spills and waste, extend the life cycle of products, serve new segments of customers, and generate long-term shareholder value. A key idea behind the circular business models is to create loops throughout to recapture value that would otherwise be lost.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Of particular concern is the irrevocable loss of raw materials due to their increase in <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> in the linear business model.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lienig&#95;&amp;&#95;Bruemmer&#95;2017&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lienig_&amp;_Bruemmer_2017-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Starting with the production of waste in manufacturing, the entropy increases further by mixing and diluting materials in their manufacturing assembly, followed by corrosion and wear and tear during the usage period. At the end of the life cycle, there is an exponential increase in disorder arising from the mixing of materials in landfills.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lienig&#95;&amp;&#95;Bruemmer&#95;2017&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lienig_&amp;_Bruemmer_2017-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a result of this directionality of the entropy law, the world's resources are effectively "lost forever".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Circular development is directly linked to the circular economy and aims to build a sustainable society based on recyclable and renewable resources, to protect society from waste, and to be able to form a model that no longer considering resources as infinite.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-africa.iclei.org&#95;26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-africa.iclei.org-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This new model of economic development focuses on the production of goods and services, taking into account environmental and social costs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-africa.iclei.org&#95;26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-africa.iclei.org-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Circular development, therefore, supports the circular economy to create new societies in line with new waste management and sustainability objectives that meet the needs of citizens. It is about enabling economies and societies, in general, to become more sustainable.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>However, critiques of the circular economy<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> suggest that proponents of the circular economy may overstate the potential benefits of the circular economy. These critiques put forward the idea that the circular economy has too many definitions to be delimited, making it an umbrella concept that, although exciting and appealing, is hard to understand and assess. Critiques mean that the literature ignores much-established knowledge. In particular, it neglects the thermodynamic principle that one can neither create nor destroy matter. Therefore, a future where waste no longer exists, where material loops are closed, and products are recycled indefinitely is, in any practical sense, impossible. They point out that a lack of inclusion of indigenous discourses from the Global South means that the conversation is less eco-centric than it depicts itself. There is a lack of clarity as to whether the circular economy is more sustainable than the linear economy and what its social benefits might be, in particular, due to diffuse contours.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other issues include the increasing risks of <a href="/wiki/Cascading_failure" title="Cascading failure">cascading failures</a> which are a feature of highly <a href="/wiki/Interdependent_networks" title="Interdependent networks">interdependent systems</a>, and have potential harm to the general public. When implemented in <a href="/wiki/Bad_faith" title="Bad faith">bad faith</a>, touting "circular economy" activities may be used for <a href="/wiki/Reputation_management" title="Reputation management">reputation</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Impression_management" title="Impression management">impression management</a> for <a href="/wiki/Public_relations" title="Public relations">public relations</a> purposes by large corporations and other vested interests; constituting a new form of <a href="/wiki/Greenwashing" title="Greenwashing">greenwashing</a>. It may thus not be the cure-all many had hoped for.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Sustainability">Sustainability</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sustainability"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">Sustainability</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Car_life_cycle.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Car_life_cycle.svg/250px-Car_life_cycle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Car_life_cycle.svg/500px-Car_life_cycle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1627" data-file-height="1627" /></a><figcaption>Typical <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">ICE</a> vehicle life cycle</figcaption></figure>
<p>In theory, the circular economy should be more <a href="/wiki/Sustainable" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable">sustainable</a> than the current linear economic system. Reducing the resources used and the waste and leakage created conserves resources and helps to reduce environmental pollution. However, some argue that these assumptions are simplistic and disregard existing systems' complexity and potential trade-offs. For example, the social dimension of sustainability seems to be only marginally addressed in many publications on the circular economy. Some cases might require different or additional strategies, like purchasing new, more energy-efficient equipment. Reviewing literature, a team of researchers from Cambridge and TU Delft showed that there are at least eight different relationship types between sustainability and the circular economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer&#95;757–768&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer_757–768-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, it is important to underline the innovation aspect at the heart of sustained development based on circular economy components.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hysa&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2020&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hysa_et_al_2020-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Scope">Scope</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scope"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The circular economy can have a broad scope. Researchers have focused on different areas such as industrial applications with both product-oriented and natural resources and services,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranta-2018&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranta-2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> practices and policies<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to better understand the limitations that the CE currently faces, strategic management for details of the circular economy and different outcomes such as potential re-use applications<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and waste management.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The circular economy includes products, infrastructure, equipment, services and buildings <sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and applies to every industry sector. It includes 'technical' resources (metals, minerals, fossil resources) and 'biological' resources (food, fibres, timber, etc.).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EMF-2012&#95;33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMF-2012-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most schools of thought advocate a shift from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> to the use of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>, and emphasize the role of diversity as a characteristic of resilient and sustainable systems. The circular economy includes a discussion of the role of money and finance as part of the wider debate, and some of its pioneers have called for a revamp of economic performance measurement tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NEWS.in-2021&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWS.in-2021-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One study points out how modularization could become a cornerstone to enabling a circular economy and enhancing the sustainability of energy infrastructure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One example of a circular economy model is the implementation of renting models in traditional ownership areas (e.g., electronics, clothes, furniture, transportation). By renting the same product to several clients, manufacturers can increase revenues per unit, thus decreasing the need to produce more to increase revenues. Recycling initiatives are often described as circular economy and are likely to be the most widespread models.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NEWS.in-2021&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWS.in-2021-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The organization Circle Economy reported that global implementation of circular economy can reduce global emissions by 22.8 billion tons, 39% of global emissions in the year 2019.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Circle&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Circle-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2050, 9.3 billion metric tons ofCO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> equivalent, or almost half of the global greenhouse gas emissions from the production of goods, might be reduced by implementing circular economy strategies in only five significant industries: cement, aluminum, steel, plastics, and food. That would equal to eliminating all current emissions caused by transportation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ellenmacarthurfoundation.org&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ellenmacarthurfoundation.org-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-EIB-2023&#95;15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIB-2023-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Background">Background</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As early as 1966, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Boulding" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth Boulding">Kenneth Boulding</a> raised awareness of an "open economy" with unlimited input resources and output sinks, in contrast with a "closed economy", in which resources and sinks are tied and remain as long as possible part of the economy. Boulding's essay "The Economics of the Coming Spaceship Earth"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Boulding1966&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boulding1966-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is often cited as the first expression of the "circular economy",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Allwood2014&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allwood2014-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although Boulding does not use that phrase.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The circular economy is grounded in the study of <a href="/wiki/Feedback_loop" class="mw-redirect" title="Feedback loop">feedback</a>-rich (<a href="/wiki/Non-linear_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-linear system">non-linear</a>) systems, particularly <a href="/wiki/Living_systems" title="Living systems">living systems</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-EMF-2012&#95;33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EMF-2012-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The contemporary understanding of the circular economy and its practical applications to economic systems has evolved, incorporating different features and contributions from a variety of concepts sharing the idea of closed loops. Some of the relevant theoretical influences are <a href="/wiki/Cradle_to_cradle" class="mw-redirect" title="Cradle to cradle">cradle to cradle</a>, laws of ecology (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Barry_Commoner#The_Closing_Circle" title="Barry Commoner">Barry Commoner §&#160;The Closing Circle</a>), looped and performance economy (<a href="/wiki/Walter_R._Stahel" title="Walter R. Stahel">Walter R. Stahel</a>), <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_design" title="Regenerative design">regenerative design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_ecology" title="Industrial ecology">industrial ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomimicry" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomimicry">biomimicry</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Economy" title="The Blue Economy">blue economy</a> (see section "Related concepts").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer&#95;757–768&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer_757–768-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The circular economy was further modelled by British environmental economists <a href="/wiki/David_W._Pearce" class="mw-redirect" title="David W. Pearce">David W. Pearce</a> and R. Kerry Turner in 1989. In <i>Economics of Natural Resources and the Environment</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> they pointed out that a traditional open-ended economy was developed with no built-in tendency to recycle, which was reflected by treating the environment as a waste reservoir.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Tim_Jackson_(economist)" title="Tim Jackson (economist)">Tim Jackson</a> began to create the scientific basis for this new approach to industrial production in his edited collection <i>Clean Production Strategies</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including chapters from preeminent writers in the field such as <a href="/wiki/Walter_R._Stahel" title="Walter R. Stahel">Walter R Stahel</a>, Bill Rees and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Costanza" title="Robert Costanza">Robert Constanza</a>. At the time still called 'preventive environmental management', his follow-on book <i><a href="/wiki/Material_Concerns" title="Material Concerns">Material Concerns: Pollution, Profit and Quality of Life</a></i> synthesized these findings into a manifesto for change, moving industrial production away from an extractive linear system towards a more <i>circular</i> economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Emergence_of_the_idea">Emergence of the idea</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Emergence of the idea"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Walter Stahel and Genevieve Reday in a 1976 research report to the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, "The Potential for Substituting Manpower for Energy", sketched the vision of an economy in loops (or a circular economy) and its impact on <a href="/wiki/Job_creation" class="mw-redirect" title="Job creation">job creation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">economic competitiveness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resource_consumption" title="Resource consumption">resource savings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waste_prevention" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste prevention">waste prevention</a>. The report was published in 1982 as the book <i>Jobs for Tomorrow: The Potential for Substituting Manpower for Energy</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Considered one of the first pragmatic and credible sustainability <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tanks</a>, the main goals of Stahel's institute are to extend the working life of products, to make goods last longer, to reuse existing goods, and ultimately to prevent waste. This model emphasizes the importance of <a href="/wiki/Service_economy" title="Service economy">selling services</a> rather than products, an idea referred to as the "functional service economy" and sometimes put under the wider notion of "performance economy." This model also advocates "more localization of economic activity".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Promoting a circular economy was identified as a national policy in <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_China#Eleventh_Plan_(2006–2010)" title="Five-year plans of China">China's 11th five-year plan</a> starting in 2006.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ellen_MacArthur_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellen MacArthur Foundation">Ellen MacArthur Foundation</a> has more recently outlined the economic opportunity of a circular economy, bringing together complementary schools of thought in an attempt to create a coherent framework, thus giving the concept a wide exposure and appeal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most frequently described as a framework for thinking, its supporters claim it is a coherent model that has value as part of a response to the end of the era of <a href="/wiki/Oil_price" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil price">cheap oil</a> and materials and, moreover, contributes to the transition to a <a href="/wiki/Low-carbon_economy" title="Low-carbon economy">low-carbon economy</a>. In line with this, a circular economy can contribute to meeting the COP 21 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a>. The emissions reduction commitments made by 195 countries at the COP 21 Paris Agreement are not sufficient to limit global warming to 1.5&#160;°C. To reach the 1.5&#160;°C ambition, it is estimated that additional emissions reductions of 15 billion tonnes of CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> per year need to be achieved by 2030. Circle Economy and Ecofys estimated that circular economy strategies may deliver emissions reductions that could bridge the gap by half.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Moving_away_from_the_linear_model">Moving away from the linear model</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Moving away from the linear model"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Linear "take, make, dispose" <a href="/wiki/List_of_industrial_processes" class="mw-redirect" title="List of industrial processes">industrial processes</a>, and the lifestyles dependent on them, use up finite reserves to create products with a finite lifespan, which end up in <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Incinerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Incinerator">incinerators</a>. The circular approach, by contrast, takes insights from living systems. It considers that our systems should work like organisms, processing nutrients that can be fed back into the cycle—whether biological or technical—hence the "closed loop" or "regenerative" terms usually associated with it. The generic circular economy label can be applied to or claimed by several different schools of thought, but all of them gravitate around the same basic principles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>One prominent thinker is <a href="/wiki/Walter_R._Stahel" title="Walter R. Stahel">Walter R. Stahel</a>, an architect and economist who is "generally regarded as the father of circular economy" and industrial sustainability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Having also been credited with coining the expression "Cradle to Cradle" (in contrast with "Cradle to Grave", illustrating our "Resource to Waste" way of functioning), in the late 1970s, Stahel worked on developing a "closed loop" approach to production processes, co-founding the Product-Life Institute in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. In the UK, Steve D. Parker researched waste as a resource in the UK agricultural sector in 1982, developing novel closed-loop production systems. These systems mimicked and worked with the biological ecosystems they exploited.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="&quot;Re-&quot;_models_to_rank_circularity_priority"><span id=".22Re-.22_models_to_rank_circularity_priority"></span>"Re-" models to rank circularity priority</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: &quot;Re-&quot; models to rank circularity priority"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm/250px-seek%3D383-Towards_a_Circular_City.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="3470" data-mwtitle="Towards_a_Circular_City.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Towards_a_Circular_City.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm.480p.vp9.webm#t=00:06:23,00:08:30" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm#t=00:06:23,00:08:30" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm.1080p.vp9.webm#t=00:06:23,00:08:30" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="1080p.vp9.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm.144p.mjpeg.mov#t=00:06:23,00:08:30" type="video/quicktime" data-transcodekey="144p.mjpeg.mov" data-width="256" data-height="144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/82/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm/Towards_a_Circular_City.webm.240p.vp9.webm#t=00:06:23,00:08:30" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /></video></span><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Cramer" title="Jacqueline Cramer">Jacqueline Cramer</a> explaining their 10R principle in Vancouver (2017)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Economie_circulaire.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Economie_circulaire.png/250px-Economie_circulaire.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="263" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Economie_circulaire.png/500px-Economie_circulaire.png 2x" data-file-width="1618" data-file-height="1705" /></a><figcaption>A French-language model of circularity using mostly "R"s: <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">partage</i></span> (share), <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">réparation</i></span> (repair), <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">réemploi</i></span> (reemploy), <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">remise à neuf</i></span> (refurbish/renew), <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">recyclage</i></span> (recycle)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Since the 2010s, several models have been proposed to show a priority hierarchy for circular economy, namely focused on ordering actions/focuses to achieve value retention or reduction of waste and environmental impact. These models usually list a sequence of various English verbs or nouns starting with "re-"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reike&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2018&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reike_et_al_2018-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>, likely inspired by the motto "Reduce, Reuse, Recycle" which came about in the 1970s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Breteler (2022) shared a "10R principle" developed by sustainable entrepreneurship professor and former <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Housing,_Spatial_Planning_and_the_Environment_(Netherlands)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Housing, Spatial Planning and the Environment (Netherlands)">Dutch Environment Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Cramer" title="Jacqueline Cramer">Jacqueline Cramer</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2018, Denise Reike, Walter Vermeulen, and Sjors Witjes compared 69 different "R hierarchies", finding 38 distinct "re"-words, some of which represent the same concepts. They unified these as "Circular Economy 3.0", a comprehensive 10R hierarchy framework.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reike&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2018&#95;75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reike_et_al_2018-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Towards_the_circular_economy">Towards the circular economy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Towards the circular economy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2013, a report was released entitled <i>Towards the Circular Economy: Economic and Business Rationale for an Accelerated Transition</i>. The report, commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Ellen_MacArthur_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellen MacArthur Foundation">Ellen MacArthur Foundation</a> and developed by <a href="/wiki/McKinsey_%26_Company" title="McKinsey &amp; Company">McKinsey &amp; Company</a>, was the first volume of its kind<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to consider the economic and business opportunity for the transition to a restorative, circular model. Using product case studies and economy-wide analysis, the report details the potential for significant benefits across the EU. It argues that a subset of the EU manufacturing sector could realize net materials cost savings worth up to $630 billion annually towards 2025—stimulating economic activity in the areas of product development, remanufacturing and refurbishment. <i>Towards the Circular Economy</i> also identified the key building blocks in making the transition to a circular economy, namely in skills in circular design and production, new business models, skills in building cascades and reverse cycles, and cross-cycle/cross-sector collaboration.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is supported by a case study from the <a href="/wiki/Automotive_industry" title="Automotive industry">automotive industry</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schmitt-2021&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmitt-2021-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> highlighting the importance of integrating a circular model holistically within the entire value chain of a company, taking into account the interdependencies between the product, process, and system level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Another report by WRAP and the Green Alliance (called "Employment and the circular economy: job creation in a more resource efficient Britain"), done in 2015 has examined different public policy scenarios to 2030. It estimates that, with no policy change, 200,000 new jobs will be created, reducing unemployment by 54,000. A more aggressive policy scenario could create 500,000 new jobs and permanently reduce unemployment by 102,000.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The International Labour Organization predicts that implementing a circular economy by 2030 might result in an additional 7-8 million jobs being created globally.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-EIB-2023&#95;15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIB-2023-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, other research has also found that the adoption of circular economy principles may lead to job losses in emerging economies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, implementing a circular economy in the United States has been presented by Ranta et al.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranta-2018&#95;54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranta-2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who analyzed the institutional drivers and barriers for the circular economy in different regions worldwide, by following the framework developed by Scott R.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Scott-2008&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-2008-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the article, different worldwide environment-friendly institutions were selected, and two types of manufacturing processes were chosen for the analysis (1) a product-oriented, and (2) a waste management.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranta-2018&#95;54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranta-2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Scott-2008&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-2008-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Specifically, in the U.S., the product-oriented company case in the study was <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a>, a US manufacturing company for computer technology, which was the first company to offer free recycling to customers and to launch to the market a computer made from recycling materials from a verified third-party source.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranta-2018&#95;54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranta-2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Moreover, the waste management case that includes many stages such as collection, disposal, recycling<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in the study was <a href="/wiki/Republic_Services" title="Republic Services">Republic Services</a>, the second-largest waste management company in the US. The approach to defining the drivers and barriers was to first identify indicators for their cases in study and then to categorize these indicators into drivers when the indicator was in favor of the circular economy model or a barrier when it was not.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranta-2018&#95;54-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranta-2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 2 March 2022 in Nairobi, representatives of 175 countries pledged to create a legally binding agreement to end plastic pollution by the end of the year 2024. The agreement should address the full lifecycle of plastic and propose alternatives including <a href="/wiki/Reusability" title="Reusability">reusability</a>. The agreement is expected to facilitate the transition to a circular economy that will reduce GHG emissions by 25 percent, according to the published statement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that the <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">waste sector</a> can achieve net zero emissions in the coming decades by improving and adopting circular approaches to municipal solid waste systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Circularity is growing in national focus. It was one focus of the 2024 <a href="/wiki/2024_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2024 United Nations Climate Change Conference">COP 29</a> <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="United Nations Climate Change Conference">United Nations Climate Conference</a> hosted in Baku, Azerbaijan.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the annual conference, a Joint Resolution declaring the intent of the Republic of Azerbaijan to focus on global and regional collaboration focused on <a href="/wiki/Efficiency" title="Efficiency">efficiency</a> and circularity was established and signed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Circular_product_design_and_standards">Circular product design and standards</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Circular product design and standards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_design" title="Sustainable design">Sustainable design</a></div>
<p>Product designs that optimize durability, ease of maintenance and repair, upgradability, re-manufacturability, separability, disassembly, and reassembly are considered key elements for the transition toward circularity of products, though designers must balance these principles with avoiding excessive margins that can lead to overdesign and reduced overall sustainability.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Standardization#Environmental_protection" title="Standardization">Standardization</a> can facilitate related "innovative, sustainable and competitive advantages for European businesses and consumers".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Design for standardization and compatibility would make "product parts and interfaces suitable for other products and aims at multi-functionality and modularity".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A "Product Family Approach" has been proposed to establish "commonality, compatibility, standardization, or modularization among different products or product lines".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>It has been argued that emerging technologies should be designed with circular economy principles from the start, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell#Recycling" title="Solar cell">including solar panels</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Design_of_circularity_processes">Design of circularity processes</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Design of circularity processes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Agbogbloshie_Ghana.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Agbogbloshie_Ghana.jpg/250px-Agbogbloshie_Ghana.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Agbogbloshie_Ghana.jpg/500px-Agbogbloshie_Ghana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Not all types of recycling processes (one circularity process) have equal impact on health and sustainability.</figcaption></figure>
<p>For sustainability and health, the circularity process designs may be of crucial importance. Large amounts of electronic waste are already recycled but far from where they were consumed, with often low efficiency, and with substantial <a href="/wiki/Electronic_waste#Human_health_and_safety" title="Electronic waste">negative effects on human health and the foreign environment</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Recycling should therefore "reduce environmental impacts of the overall product/service provision system assessed based on the <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_assessment" title="Life-cycle assessment">life-cycle assessment</a> approach".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One study suggests that "a mandatory certification scheme for recyclers of electronic waste, in or out of Europe, would help to incentivize high-quality treatment processes and efficient material recovery".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Digitalization may enable more efficient corporate processes and minimize waste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Circular_business_models">Circular business models</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Circular_economy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Circular business models"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_business" title="Sustainable business">Sustainable business</a></div><p>
While the initial focus of the academic, industry, and policy activities was mainly focused on the development of re-X (recycling, remanufacturing, reuse, etc.) technology, it soon became clear that the technological capabilities increasingly exceed their implementation. To leverage this technology for the transition toward a circular economy, various stakeholders have to work together. This shifted attention towards business-model innovation as a key leverage for 'circular' technology adaption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rheaply" title="Rheaply">Rheaply</a>, a platform that aims to scale reuse within and between organizations, is an example of a technology that focuses on asset management &amp; disposition to support organizations transitioning to circular business models.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> </p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Circular_business_model.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Circular_business_model.png/250px-Circular_business_model.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Circular_business_model.png/500px-Circular_business_model.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="566" /></a><figcaption>Characteristic solutions applied to a business model that can make it more circular<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer2018&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer2018-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure><p>Circular business models can be defined as business models that are closing, narrowing, slowing, intensifying, and dematerializing loops, to minimize the resource inputs into and the waste and emission leakage out of the organizational system. This comprises recycling measures (closing), efficiency improvements (narrowing), use phase extensions (slowing), a more intense use phase (intensifying), and the substitution of products by service and software solutions (dematerializing).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Geissdoerfer2018&#95;109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geissdoerfer2018-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These strategies can be achieved through the purposeful design of material recovery processes and related circular supply chains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Batista&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batista-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As illustrated in the Figure, these five approaches to resource loops can also be seen as generic strategies or archetypes of circular business model innovation. The development of circular products, circular business models, and, more generally, the circular economy is conditioned upon the affordances of the materials involved, that is the enablement and constraints afforded by these materials to someone engaging with them for circular purposes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
